  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_50d54d76-4f9c-4ca5-8a22-3bf9c76670f1'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

  <span id="freeText15457158276716044855" style="display:none">Set in Paris and attracting comparisons with Franz Kafka and Edgar Allan Poe, <i>The Pigeon</i> is Patrick Süskind's tense, disturbing follow-up to the bestselling <i>Perfume. </i>The novella tells the story of a day in the meticulously ordered life of bank security guard Jonathan Noel, who has been hiding from life since his wife left him for her Tunisian lover. When Jonathan opens his front door on a day he believes will be just like any other, he encounters not the desired empty hallway but an unwelcome, diabolical intruder . . .</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/765909-i-don-t-know-if-this-book-would-do-it-for">I don&#39;t know if this book would do it for me... Because some people are saying it resembles Kafka, which I loath. The self-pitying, uninteresting character, in a story where nothing happens, uh. Metamorphosis is one of my top hated books. BUT!! I simply adored Perfume. Grenouille&#39;s mind is just fascinating to walk into. And the dark atmosphere really did it for me.
So I fell biased. Should I give it a try?</a>
</div>

Die Taube = The Pigeon, Patrick Süskind<br/><br/>The Pigeon is a novella by Patrick Süskind, about the fictional character Jonathan Noel, a solitary Parisian bank security guard, who undergoes an existential crisis when a pigeon roosts in front of his one-room apartment's door, prohibiting him entrance to his private sanctuary. <br/><br/>The story takes place in the span of one day, and follows how this seemingly insignificant event compounds to threaten Noel's sanity. The titular pigeon can be a symbol for di</span>
  <span id="freeText16270035762229924752" style="display:none">215.	Die Taube = The Pigeon, Patrick Süskind<br /><br />The Pigeon is a novella by Patrick Süskind, about the fictional character Jonathan Noel, a solitary Parisian bank security guard, who undergoes an existential crisis when a pigeon roosts in front of his one-room apartment's door, prohibiting him entrance to his private sanctuary. <br /><br />The story takes place in the span of one day, and follows how this seemingly insignificant event compounds to threaten Noel's sanity. The titular pigeon can be a symbol for disorder intruding on the protagonist's meticulously organized existence, and is obviously in ironic inter textual dialogue with U.S. writer Edgar Allan Poe's 1845 poem The Raven, which features its titular bird perched over its protagonist's door instead of M. Noel's pigeon. This book is Süskind's followup to his nine-year bestselling first novel, Perfume.<br /><br />کبوتر - پاتریک زوسکیند (مرکز، آهنگ دیگر) ادبیات؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز ششم ماه ژوئن سال 2012 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان 1: کبوتر؛ اثر: پاتریک زوسکیند؛ ترجمه: فرهاد سلیمانیان؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نشر مرکز، 1384، در 87 ص، شابک: 9643058808؛ از متن انگلیسی کتاب ترجمه شده؛ چاپ پیشین: آهنگ دیگر، 1384، موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان آلمانی، سده ی 20 م<br /><br />عنوان 2: کبوتر؛ اثر: پاتریک زوسکیند؛ برگردان: ندا کاویانی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، آهنگ دیگر، 1384، در 87 ص، شابک: 9648433151؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان آلمانی، سده ی 20 م<br /><br />یوناتان، نماد انسان امروزین است، از تنهایی به ستوه آمده، کودک کهنسالی ست، که دوست دارد احساس آرامش کند. انسانی که فارغ از همه­ ی بایدها، و نبایدها، می­خواهد آزاد زندگی کند، و هیچ تعهدی نداشته باشد. کتاب از شاهکارهای بیمانند نثر معاصر آلمان، و داستانی منسجم است که از بعد روان‌شناختی، بسیار ماهرانه روایت شده است. سبک نگارش کتاب، یادآور نوشته‌ های آغازین «پاتریشیا های اسمیت» است، اما از نظر پختگی در راستای سنت ناول‌ نویسان بزرگ در ادبیات داستانی اروپا، قرار دارد. در جریان داستان، به ندرت کنش، و جنب و جوش به چشم می‌خورد، اما ماجرا درست همانند یک تندباد، خوانشگر را در‌برمی‌گیرد. خوانشگر داستان، نه تنها ماجرا را استشمام می‌کند، بلکه آنچه را نویسنده شرح می‌دهد، با تمام وجود می‌چشد، می‌بیند، و می‌شنود. «پاتریک زوسکیند» هنرمندی ست، که اگر لازم باشد، قادر است یک زندگی پیش پا افتاده، و محقر را، با در‌و‌نمایه‌ ی دراماتیک با شکوه، توصیف کند. ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18357151569319036609"><b>What Passes For Success</b><br/><br/><i>“He was not a man of action. He was a man of resignation.”</i> And consequently, he was also a man of strict routine, of rigid habit, of acute sensitivity to any deviation from the expected. Suskind’s anti-protagonist is also emotional; he suffers anger and fear, and resentments. <br/><br/>But these emotions are provoked only by events, mostly trivial except to him, not by memory or positive desire of any sort. He wants nothing other than what he has, especially when the little he has i</span>
  <span id="freeText18357151569319036609" style="display:none"><b>What Passes For Success</b><br /><br /><i>“He was not a man of action. He was a man of resignation.”</i> And consequently, he was also a man of strict routine, of rigid habit, of acute sensitivity to any deviation from the expected. Suskind’s anti-protagonist is also emotional; he suffers anger and fear, and resentments. <br /><br />But these emotions are provoked only by events, mostly trivial except to him, not by memory or positive desire of any sort. He wants nothing other than what he has, especially when the little he has is threatened. <br /><br />Until the moment of self-recognition, the moment when his whole life is explained in an instant as one trapped in childhood trauma: <i>“... you’re a child, you only dreamed that you had grown up to be a disgusting old guard in Paris, but you’re a child and you’re sitting in the cellar of your parents’ house, and outside is war, and you’re trapped, buried, forgotten. Why don’t they come? Why don’t they rescue me? Why is it so deathly still? Where are the other people? My God, where are the other people? I simply cannot live without other people!”</i> <br /><br />Learning how to survive without other people had become not just a necessary tactic but an end in itself. The Dutch build more polders, perhaps simply because they can; South Americans have revolutions because they know how; Americans arm teachers with guns because... well just because. To what degree do all of us find ourselves similarly trapped by the successful tactics of youth?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13239764927485755895">Hunt Before Being Hunted<br/><br/><br/>Jonathan is a NO man -- no friends, no family, no pets, ... no nothing!<br/>His tomorrows are replicas of yesterdays, turning his life into a long, boring, monotonous sort of tune...<br/><br/>Until that fatal pigeon day!<br/><br/>The moment he set eyes on that infamous pigeon in the corridor, chaos popped in!<br/>His shelter was no longer safe -- it was mandatory to leave it ... fast and furiously!<br/><br/>Therefore, assaulted by an emotional turmoil, he fled!...<br/><br/>However, the unknown was out there, waiting in e</span>
  <span id="freeText13239764927485755895" style="display:none">Hunt Before Being Hunted<br /><br /><br />Jonathan is a NO man -- no friends, no family, no pets, ... no nothing!<br />His tomorrows are replicas of yesterdays, turning his life into a long, boring, monotonous sort of tune...<br /><br />Until that fatal pigeon day!<br /><br />The moment he set eyes on that infamous pigeon in the corridor, chaos popped in!<br />His shelter was no longer safe -- it was mandatory to leave it ... fast and furiously!<br /><br />Therefore, assaulted by an emotional turmoil, he fled!...<br /><br />However, the unknown was out there, waiting in every corner, menacing him, assuming all kinds of dangers that had to be faced...<br /><br />What could he do to get his neck out of such a menacing picture?<br /><br />Like in Paul's Simon song, "the answer is easy if we take it logically: ;)<br /><br />If he wanted freedom that much, it was time to face the pigeon!!!...<br /><br />When it takes to fears, life is a merciless hunter:<br /><br />One fine day, one of those that we left untouched, rolling and crawling out there, boldly emerges from the bunch, and annoyingly starts knocking our door, until we find no other option, except frontally facing it!...<br /><br />Therefore, as a prevention measure, here goes my hint:<br />Better start hunting your own fears, just in case they'll keep landing on your corridor, in a long terrifying queue!... ;)<br /><br />Thanks a lot "Pigeon", for leading me into such a conclusion! ;)<br />We tend to forget this simple (useful) sort of things! ;)</span>

<div id="review_300113114" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/300113114" />
    <a title="Michael Fierce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6880123-michael-fierce"><img alt="Michael Fierce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602282713p2/6880123.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300113114?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Fierce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Fierce" href="/user/show/6880123-michael-fierce">Michael Fierce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671667702" href="/book/show/1122677.The_Pigeon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=cultural-france">cultural-france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=poe-influenced">poe-influenced</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=magritte">magritte</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=kafkaesque">kafkaesque</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=cover-love">cover-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6880123-michael-fierce?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer300113114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18307369282531358967"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425766255i/13931429._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='625' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>A simple tale in novella form about Jonathan Noel, a man living in France who suffers from a neurosis that makes him afraid of breaking away from his habitual lifestyle, and who never mingles with others, unless it is absolutely necessary.<br/><br/>Once his routine is broken up by the seemingly harmless appearance of a pigeon outside his apartment door, he nearly breaks down completely. <br/><pre><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486957732i/21964119._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='533' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/></pre>
<b><i>Some people see red. Jonathan Noel sees pigeons</i></b><br/><br/>Though an inferior work to his masterpiece, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1400098.Perfume' title='Perfume by Patrick Süskind' rel='nofollow noopener'>Perfume: The Story of a Murd</a></b></span>
  <span id="freeText18307369282531358967" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425766255i/13931429._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="625" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>A simple tale in novella form about Jonathan Noel, a man living in France who suffers from a neurosis that makes him afraid of breaking away from his habitual lifestyle, and who never mingles with others, unless it is absolutely necessary.<br><br>Once his routine is broken up by the seemingly harmless appearance of a pigeon outside his apartment door, he nearly breaks down completely. <br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486957732i/21964119._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="533" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>Some people see red. Jonathan Noel sees pigeons</i></b><br><br>Though an inferior work to his masterpiece, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1400098.Perfume" title="Perfume by Patrick Süskind" rel="nofollow noopener">Perfume: The Story of a Murderer </a> - my favorite stand-alone novel of all-time - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/39402.Patrick_S_skind" title="Patrick Süskind" rel="nofollow noopener">Patrick Süskind</a> tells his short tale with a Master's touch, conveying the right mood, tone, and sense of unease needed to put one's feet right in the character's shoes.<br><br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486958228i/21964140.jpg" width="324" height="407" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>~~~~~OK...maybe not these shoes but...~~~~~</i></b><br><br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486958427i/21964150.jpg" width="324" height="277" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>~~~~~~~~...maybe these?~~~~~~~</i></b><br><br>I enjoyed it but feel it necessary to say that this is a book many won't care for. It is based off of one idea, probably could have been a bit better if it were even shorter in length, and with not a lot going on aside from mostly daily routines, it leaves one feeling there could have been more to it, maybe. I expected it to be more of a tragedy, say, in the way <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13072.Patrick_McGrath" title="Patrick McGrath" rel="nofollow noopener">Patrick McGrath's</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/973459.Spider" title="Spider by Patrick McGrath" rel="nofollow noopener">Spider</a> was - which in some ways it reminded me of - but that was obviously not the intent of the author's. I must admit, however, that I might have liked it just a little bit more if it had've been more tragic in nature, or at least ended as such.<br><br>Not to say he didn't paint a pretty bleak picture. He did. One with a pigeon at the center of it.<br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486959091i/21964190._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>What!? I got a pigeon thing going on. What of it..?</i></b><br><br>Similarly, like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/973459.Spider" title="Spider by Patrick McGrath" rel="nofollow noopener">Spider</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1122677.The_Pigeon" title="The Pigeon by Patrick Süskind" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pigeon</a>, because of its neurosis elements and its masterful authorial writing stamp clearly embedded in each word and idea, it feels almost <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Poe-like</a> in nature. Not to say it copies him, but it definitely reflects a side of him many are familiar with. <br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486959613i/21964215._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="530" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>Aww poo -I mean Poe! How am I s'posed to comb my mustache this way?</i></b><br><br>For those of you interested in reading this, just remember not to expect too much from it. I, myself, am glad to have read this and was excited to find a very good copy of this particular edition, with its wonderful tribute cover to surrealist painter, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/114616.Ren__Magritte" title="René Magritte" rel="nofollow noopener">René Magritte</a>. I found it very fitting both before and after my reading of this! <br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486960273i/21964251._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre>
<b><i>~~~~~~~~~~~~~Holeeee birdshitte!~~~~~~~~~~~~~~</i></b><br><br>*Recommended for fans of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/39402.Patrick_S_skind" title="Patrick Süskind" rel="nofollow noopener">Patrick Süskind</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13072.Patrick_McGrath" title="Patrick McGrath" rel="nofollow noopener">Patrick McGrath</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/232932.Nikolai_Gogol" title="Nikolai Gogol" rel="nofollow noopener">Nikolai Gogol</a>, and even, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/114616.Ren__Magritte" title="René Magritte" rel="nofollow noopener">René Magritte</a>.<br><pre><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486960760i/21964262.jpg" width="400" height="327" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></pre></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="18307369282531358967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_224879879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/224879879" />
    <a title="Bonnie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5136858-bonnie"><img alt="Bonnie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604440764p2/5136858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/224879879?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie" href="/user/show/5136858-bonnie">Bonnie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=shorties">shorties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=1001">1001</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=owned-book">owned-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer224879879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7710673428246291608"><i>‘At the time the pigeon affair overtook him, unhinging his life from one day to the next, Jonathan Noel, already past fifty, could look back over a good twenty-year period of total uneventfulness and would never have expected anything of importance could ever overtake him again – other than death some day.’</i><br/><br/>‘The Pigeon’ is an incredibly short story detailing a day (albeit a rather momentous day) in the life of Jonathan Noel. Jonathan leads a secluded and private life as a bank security guard in P</span>
  <span id="freeText7710673428246291608" style="display:none"><i>‘At the time the pigeon affair overtook him, unhinging his life from one day to the next, Jonathan Noel, already past fifty, could look back over a good twenty-year period of total uneventfulness and would never have expected anything of importance could ever overtake him again – other than death some day.’</i><br /><br />‘The Pigeon’ is an incredibly short story detailing a day (albeit a rather momentous day) in the life of Jonathan Noel. Jonathan leads a secluded and private life as a bank security guard in Paris. He enjoys the life he has made for himself and is perfectly content with it continuing as such for his remaining years; however, on his way to work one morning this all comes collapsing down around him as he discovers a pigeon on his front porch. As soon as the pigeon entered his life, his life literally came crumbling apart in his mind. All of his carefully made plans became as fragile as a snowflake.<br /><br /><i>‘…but he suddenly no longer saw himself – that is, he no longer saw himself as a part of the world surrounding him. It was rather, as if for a few seconds he were standing far away, outside it, and were regarding this world through the wrong end of a telescope.’</i><br /><br />I became an instant fan of Patrick Süskind after stumbling upon his novel ‘Pefume’. It left such a permanent imprint on me and is still one of my favorite books to date. I’m not sure why I never looked into whether or not he had any additional works, but after embarking on my ‘1001 Books to Read Before I Die’ reading challenge I discovered ‘The Pigeon’ as one of those 1001. Overjoyed, I knew I had to have it. <br /><br />Patrick Süskind’s writing is so thoroughly impressionable that earlier this afternoon I saw a pigeon on the side of the road and had to suppress a shiver as Jonathan’s fears flooded my mind. Mildly amusing, but, I’m not sure I’ll be able to look at a pigeon the same again. His descriptions of the pigeon and Jonathan’s instant anxiety over the pigeon were immediately understandable even though, looking at the bigger picture, it seemed as if he made a fuss over nothing. I’ll admit, I laughed at first because it seemed quite absurd, but as the story progressed you can see now it’s not just the pigeon that affected poor Jonathan in that manner; it was just the catalyst to a series of events that disrupted his painstakingly normal existence. <br /><br />I’m giving ‘The Pigeon’ 5 stars for one reason and one reason only (and it’s not because it’s as great a story as Perfume because it isn’t): because he’s a truly amazing writer. I will read anything written by Patrick Süskind. It’s just such a shame that there aren’t more novels of his in existence to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7710673428246291608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_165526344" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/165526344" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/165526344?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394563152" href="/book/show/764958.The_Pigeon">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer165526344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9176746454395429527">Jonathan Noel and his sister were abandoned by their parents when they were small so a distant uncle, who they met for the first time, brought them up. During the war, his uncle hid them in the forest. When he was old enough, he joined the army and fought in Indochina. Then he came back, found that his sister has married and left without telling him. He married a pregnant girl who later abandoned him and ran away with her Tunisian lover. <br/> <br/><b>
  <i>"...he came to the conclusion that you cannot depend o</i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText9176746454395429527" style="display:none">Jonathan Noel and his sister were abandoned by their parents when they were small so a distant uncle, who they met for the first time, brought them up. During the war, his uncle hid them in the forest. When he was old enough, he joined the army and fought in Indochina. Then he came back, found that his sister has married and left without telling him. He married a pregnant girl who later abandoned him and ran away with her Tunisian lover. <br /> <br /><b>
  <i>"...he came to the conclusion that you cannot depend on people, and that you can live in peace only if you keep them at arm's length."</i>
</b><br /><br />So he went to the city (Paris) and worked as a bank security guard. He rented a small room and its smallness, i.e., <i>keeping at arm’s length</i>, provided him the security. This daily routine of room-bank-store-room provided the stability and love that he did not feel all his life. For 30 years in that set up, he was happy. Until one morning, on his way to the common toilet, he saw a <b>pigeon</b> and it scared him to death.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>”No human being can go on living in the same house with a pigeon, a pigeon is the epitomy of chaos and anarchy, a pigeon that whizzes around unpredictably, that sets it's claws in you, picks at your eyes.."</i>
</b><br /><br />And it made him very scared and he retreated back to his bed thinking that he was having a heart attack. His secured small world came falling down.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>”How quickly the apparently solidly laid foundation of one's existence could crumble."</i>
</b><br /><br />What follows are how he tries to bring back the order in his life. All of these only in one day. A smallish 77-page book. A 52-y/o man, almost a simpleton, living in a small room with an ordinary dream (of retiring at the age of 60 with an expensive wine already waiting beneath his bed). You feel the claustrophobia and the absurdity of life. This book is being compared to Kafka (for the mystical symbolisms in <i>The Trial</i>) and to Edgar Allan Poe (the use of a bird as a predominant character in <i>The Raven</i> ). But I think the comparisons end there. Suskind in this book has the exactness of Don DeLillo in <i>The Body Artist</i> or Alessando Barrico’s <i>Silk</i> and the room as a metaphor reminds me of James Baldwin’s <i>Giovanni’s Room</i> or Emma Donaghue’s <i>Room</i>.<br /><br />I was not able to identify myself with Jonathan Noel so there is no personal trivia this time ha ha but the Suskind's prose is engaging. That's the reason why I included some striking quotes in this review. <br /><br />But my favorite of them all is this:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Walking soothes. There is a healing power in walking. The regular placement of one foot in front of the other while at the same time rowing rhythmically with the arms, the rising rate of respiration, the slight stimulation of the pulse, the actions required of eye and ear for determining direction and maintaining balance, the feeling of the passing air brushing against the skin - all these are events that mass about the body and mind in a quite irresistible fashion and allow the soul, be it ever so atrophied and bruised, to grow and expand.</i>
</b><br /><br />And I have to remember, that I have to read is more popular book, <i>Perfume: The Story of a Murderer</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9176746454395429527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1462902916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3977912867796029059">A late middle-aged bank guard is startled by a pigeon in the hallway of his dumpy rooming house. We are told in the story that the pigeon is the epitome of chaos and anarchy. This leads to a chain of events where he first becomes introspective and then disillusioned about his whole life. We assume he is Jewish because the only early memories he has are of his mother and father disappearing and of being shuffled among distant relations to be hidden. After a bad marriage that lasted only a few mon</span>
  <span id="freeText3977912867796029059" style="display:none">A late middle-aged bank guard is startled by a pigeon in the hallway of his dumpy rooming house. We are told in the story that the pigeon is the epitome of chaos and anarchy. This leads to a chain of events where he first becomes introspective and then disillusioned about his whole life. We assume he is Jewish because the only early memories he has are of his mother and father disappearing and of being shuffled among distant relations to be hidden. After a bad marriage that lasted only a few months, he gave up on life. For decades he has spent his days standing on three marble steps outside a bank building. And every night for decades he ate food off a hotplate and watched TV in his room. He has no family, avoids people, and is absolutely friendless. Yet, as he reflects on the lives of homeless men he sees in the streets, he thinks things could be worse. Where do we go from here? Not a masterpiece but thought-provoking and a quick read – only 115 pages. The author is better known for the much darker novel, Perfume, the story of an 18th century mass murderer that was made into a movie. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3977912867796029059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_19181836" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19181836" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616393-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349780944p2/616393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19181836?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/616393-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">kafka fans, paranoid people</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616393-julia?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616393-julia?shelf=psychological">psychological</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19181836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6801285782317643675">For fans of Kafka, this one is for you. It's written in deceptively simply language and full of paranoia and self-loathing. The main character seems at first forgettable but quickly descends into a personal and claustrophobic hell. <br/><br/>I'm not fully sure why this was the perfect book for the moment, but I was completely absorbed. This book is perfect for waiting at the airport or any impersonal place where you feel alone. </span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1678727066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1678727066" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1678727066?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1678727066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10781505358782990608">Jonathan Noel is a French bank guard who, for decades, has lived a very orderly and timid, almost non-existence which is one day shattered when a pigeon appears in his apartment building’s hallway. Patrick Suskind’s novella takes the reader through a day in this quiet man’s life as his mind unravels…<br/><br/>I really liked Suskind’s Perfume but his follow-up, The Pigeon, is underwhelming. Comparisons to Kafka and Poe are a bit much but this is still a well-written story even if it’s not the most gripping</span>
  <span id="freeText10781505358782990608" style="display:none">Jonathan Noel is a French bank guard who, for decades, has lived a very orderly and timid, almost non-existence which is one day shattered when a pigeon appears in his apartment building’s hallway. Patrick Suskind’s novella takes the reader through a day in this quiet man’s life as his mind unravels…<br /><br />I really liked Suskind’s Perfume but his follow-up, The Pigeon, is underwhelming. Comparisons to Kafka and Poe are a bit much but this is still a well-written story even if it’s not the most gripping. From his nightmarish encounter with the pigeon, Jonathan Noel misses opening the gate for his boss’s limo - his only real responsibility in his monotonous bank guard job - watches a clochard (French homeless man) on the street, and tears his trousers. Where do writers get their ideas, eh? <br /><br />But obviously there’s subtext to all of this. We learn from the beginning that Jonathan Noel’s parents were killed by the Nazis in WW2 and that his wife left him for another man - it’s likely that the character’s deliberately isolated, routine and dull life is a reaction to that trauma. If he’s alone, if he doesn’t rely on anyone, he can’t be hurt by abandonment. The introduction of the pigeon upsets his delicate equilibrium and forces him into action, something he’s avoided doing almost his entire life. <br /><br />Maybe Suskind is suggesting that we shouldn’t let fear (represented by the pigeon) stand in our way from living a full life - that reaching out to people, while leading to uncertainty, is a necessary step on the way to happiness and that a little chaos is always needed? Maybe he’s pointing out that the trauma the Nazis inflicted on Europe echoes through the years? Maybe it’s just a character portrait without a message? <br /><br />Either way, the novella doesn’t leave much of an impression and at times its knowing literariness compounds that obliqueness: I noticed the parallels between the pigeon and the clochard pooping, Jonathan Noel’s meal of fish, bread, fruit and wine is like the clochard’s, and he walks through the rain barefoot as a child and then later with shoes as an adult. All fine and good but I don’t understand the parallels, except maybe the barefoot/shoes thing - the shoes are a barrier between him and the outside world reinforcing how protected he is now compared to how carefree he once was. I feel like the novella’s trying to say something more but it doesn’t achieve it. <br /><br />The Pigeon is well-written and that alone keeps the reader engaged through Suskind’s psychological character portrait regardless of the mundane anti-story. But it’s not the most fascinating subject matter and the unfocused, meandering nature of the narrative left me unsatisfied and unsure of what to make of it as a whole. The Pigeon is certainly unusual but fans of Perfume shouldn’t expect the same kind of brilliance in this slighter effort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10781505358782990608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2967683133" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2967683133" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2967683133?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394563152" href="/book/show/764958.The_Pigeon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2967683133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16741287303113402561">Flowing, quick, moving short novel about a man who sought and achieved solitude and stability after being abandoned by his parents (who were sent to Nazi camps) and later his wife, living in an 81-square-foot room in Paris, working as a guard standing outside a bank his entire career, until one day he emerges from the shared bathroom down the hall from his room and sees a pigeon and pretty much loses his shit, becomes self-doubtful, confrontational, self-conscious, distracted, questions every pr</span>
  <span id="freeText16741287303113402561" style="display:none">Flowing, quick, moving short novel about a man who sought and achieved solitude and stability after being abandoned by his parents (who were sent to Nazi camps) and later his wife, living in an 81-square-foot room in Paris, working as a guard standing outside a bank his entire career, until one day he emerges from the shared bathroom down the hall from his room and sees a pigeon and pretty much loses his shit, becomes self-doubtful, confrontational, self-conscious, distracted, questions every presumption about how he's lived his life, totally freaks out about a tear in the fabric of his uniform akin to one ripping through the universe, considers suicide, but after an enormous thunderstorm hovering over the city like an occupying army he seems to regain his shit and recovers. Wrote the general gist just now to remember later on since I read this in four sittings -- train to work, at lunch, train home, at night in bed -- and might not remember much of it in the future. Loved that it ended well. The page description of the leaden thunderstorm's increasing tension and rumblings was worth the sticker price. The description of the "clochard" (a specifically Parisian variety of homeless man) crouched and spraying diarrhea between two cars was weirdly similar to a scene I wrote in an unpublished novel involving a guy shitting on a crowded NYC subway platform. Suskind can be such a sensual writer it seems -- really excels at description, writing about food, that sort of thing. Loved how this one changes registers as Jonathan gets worked up, how the sentences lengthen and loosen. Otherwise, at one point I registered two Kafka precedents, nearly on the same page: his work as a guard suggested something of The Hunger Artist and when compared to the sphinx it suggested Before the Law, the parable at the end of The Trial about the guard with the Tartan beard impeding K's entry to the Law. The opening line seems a little Metamorphosis-y too but this is pitched in post-war Paris and as such at times his dissolution suggested maybe something about the general societal upheaval in the '60s and '70s? As with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2893052200" rel="nofollow noopener"> Perfume</a>, which I loved, it's light-handed when it comes to theme -- it's suggestive, or there's a sense that there's significance there if you seek it but it's not essential at all to enjoyment of either book, both of which succeed on the levels of story and language and character and even plot (what happens next). This one does explicitly refer to WWII and the Holocaust though but only in the beginning and end. A PTSD novel, really. I'll definitely read some more Suskind soon and wish I'd read him long ago. Also: John E. Woods, the translator, is easily one of the finest prose writers of the late 20th century. Becoming a John E. Woods completist seems like a reasonable goal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16741287303113402561" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2332302382057177883" style="display:none">"A really short novel that packs a punch. Jonathan Noel leads an ordered life, nothing spectacular but everything is just the way he wants it and has been for decades. He has a job and an apartment in Paris and a little bit of money although not a lot. He goes about his day in an ordered fashion, keeping to himself, away from others and any other events. A perfect and simple life.<br /><br />After decades Jonathan has a plan, not a grand plan, not a fanciful plan but one that will see him even more settled into his ordinary, straightforward, mundane life. This plan will come to complete fruition at the end of the year and Jonathan thinks about it often. In fact Jonathan is thinking about it today, as he prepares for work.<br /><br />There is a pig<br /> eon just outside his door.<br /><br />How can a man be so neurotic as to allow his simple and ordered life be destroyed by the smallest of encounters? A man who has everything in place, has simplistic but REAL plans, never strays from the day to day but he is happy, happier than most - or a least happier than some of those he encounters on a daily basis in his line of work. Yet such an insignificant encounter turns his life on its head.<br /><br />How far will he allow his life to spiral and what would it take to bring it back on track?<br /><br /><br /><br />"</span>

<div id="review_3413613278" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer7960883391190135980">Exceptional, strange, offbeat and absurd, novel in which a man quickly saw a midlife crisis. Not that famous episode of "midlife crisis", not an emergency related to the meeting with a common bird. This story based on panic of a man in his ordinary fifties, routine, mute, faced with the presence of a pigeon in the corridor leading to his room. The action of the book takes place over a brief period, which the author indulges in a psychological study of his character. I think that this book, becau</span>
  <span id="freeText7960883391190135980" style="display:none">Exceptional, strange, offbeat and absurd, novel in which a man quickly saw a midlife crisis. Not that famous episode of "midlife crisis", not an emergency related to the meeting with a common bird. This story based on panic of a man in his ordinary fifties, routine, mute, faced with the presence of a pigeon in the corridor leading to his room. The action of the book takes place over a brief period, which the author indulges in a psychological study of his character. I think that this book, because of its strangeness, can't leave the reader indifferent. Well written text properly conducted history. A book to discover, not if only out of curiosity.</span>

<div id="review_2168147116" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2168147116" />
    <a title="তানজীম Rahman)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35042455-rahman"><img alt="তানজীম Rahman)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478579683p2/35042455.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2168147116?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="তানজীম Rahman)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="তানজীম Rahman)" href="/user/show/35042455-rahman">তানজীম Rahman)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2168147116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17953807231392600648">The book can be read as a kind of anti-life manual. Instructions on how-not-to-live. We all often fret over things that are miniscule, let them ruin our days; let them take over our thoughts completely. We frequently don’t realize the things are minor because we are too close to them, too invested. In this book, Suskind creates a necessary distance between the person and the events, so that their minor nature can become clearer. This is what you do, he tells us. Please don’t do that, he asks. He</span>
  <span id="freeText17953807231392600648" style="display:none">The book can be read as a kind of anti-life manual. Instructions on how-not-to-live. We all often fret over things that are miniscule, let them ruin our days; let them take over our thoughts completely. We frequently don’t realize the things are minor because we are too close to them, too invested. In this book, Suskind creates a necessary distance between the person and the events, so that their minor nature can become clearer. This is what you do, he tells us. Please don’t do that, he asks. He also emphasizes that a life lived too orderly can be the principle cause of this kind of stress. The extremely detailed style of narration is also not just a stylistic choice. It reflects the meticulousness of the protagonist's habits, and reveals how the minutest of minute things can matter to him.<br />The ending goes out of its way to say that sometimes things work themselves out, so don’t waste your time worrying over them.<br />Interesting read for sure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17953807231392600648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8745727732832230933">Jonathan Noel was not fond of events that rattled his inner equilibrium<br/> <br/>His uncle had demanded that he report for military duty, and he obediently signed up for three years<br/> <br/>For the first time in his life he made a decision on his own: he withdrew his savings, packed his bag, and headed for Paris<br/> <br/>Upon entering room number 24 for the first time, he had known at once: this is it<br/><br/>This is what you’ve really always wanted, this is where you’ll stay<br/><br/>Just the way it happens to other people with love at fi</span>
  <span id="freeText8745727732832230933" style="display:none">Jonathan Noel was not fond of events that rattled his inner equilibrium<br /> <br />His uncle had demanded that he report for military duty, and he obediently signed up for three years<br /> <br />For the first time in his life he made a decision on his own: he withdrew his savings, packed his bag, and headed for Paris<br /> <br />Upon entering room number 24 for the first time, he had known at once: this is it<br /><br />This is what you’ve really always wanted, this is where you’ll stay<br /><br />Just the way it happens to other people with love at first sight<br /> <br />It would remain his island of security in a world of insecurity, his refuge, his beloved<br /><br />Yes, for she received him with a tender embrace each evening when he returned home<br /> <br />That was the state of things when the affair with the pigeon occurred<br /> <br />He could interpret every crack, every click, every soft ripple or rustle, the very silence itself<br /> <br />How do you shoot down a pigeon? It flutters around, a pigeon does, so that you can easily miss<br /> <br />No human being can go on living in the same house with a pigeon, a pigeon is the epitome of chaos and anarchy<br /> <br />In no circumstances could he live under the same roof with this pigeon, not a single day, not a single night, not a single hour<br /> <br />He could live in a hotel for months<br /> <br />How long did a pigeon live?<br /> <br />He would have to explain himself, but how could he do that? He would have to invent some lie, but what sort of lie<br /> <br />People could only assume he was crazy. Perhaps he was crazy<br /> <br />A guard was like a sphinx. He functioned not by some deed, but rather by his mere bodily presence. <br /><br />He confronted the potential robber with that and that alone<br /> <br />He had stood on the marble steps in front of the bank and kept watch for thirty years now, without fear, without self-doubt, without the least sense of discontent and without the slightest look of glumness, until today<br /> <br />He could feel the burden of his body as a painful pressure<br /> <br />Transparent veils fluttered before things<br /> <br />Nothing was left to be precisely fixed. Everything quivered<br /> <br />He was so very ashamed of himself<br /> <br />Once he had come to the realization that the essence of human freedom consisted in the possession of a shared toilet and that this essential freedom was at his disposal, he was immediately seized with a profound satisfaction<br /> <br />How quickly the apparently solidly laid foundation of one’s existence could crumble<br /> <br />It seemed to him as if he had been wounded, as if the gaping hole was not just in his trousers, but in his own flesh and that he would die of this wound if he did not close it at once<br /> <br />His raging self-hatred made him want to jump out of his own skin<br /> <br />He had a mighty urge to pull out his pistol and let loose in every direction smashing down everything and burying it all, all of it, the whole miserable, dreary, loud, stinking world: <br /><br />So universal, so titanic was his hate that afternoon, that he would have liked to reduce the world to rubble and ashes, because he had a hole in his trousers<br /> <br />But he didn’t do it, thank God, he didn’t do it<br /> <br />He was totally incapable of asserting himself in either word or deed<br /> <br />He wished that he could lose consciousness, that his knees would buckle and he would collapse<br /> <br />It seemed to him that this whole body enveloping him was no longer his, but that he, Jonathan – or what was left of him – was nothing but a tiny, shrunken gnome inside the gigantic structure of a strange body<br /><br />A helpless dwarf imprisoned in a human machine grown too large, much too complicated, one he could no longer control and bend to his own will, but which instead was controlled, if at all, by itself or by some other power<br /> <br />Walking soothes. There is a healing power in walking<br /> <br />He set out to walk to his hotel<br /> <br />He also ate slowly because he believed this meal to be his last<br /> <br />I’ll kill myself tomorrow, he said. Then he fell asleep<br /><br />That night there was a thunderstorm<br /> <br />Doomsday – an earthquake, the atom bomb, or both – at any rate the absolute end<br /> <br />Except for him nothing else existed<br /> <br />All perception, sight, hearing, sense of balance – everything that could have told him who and where he was – fell away in the perfect vacuum of darkness and stillness<br /> <br />Where are the other people? My God, where are the other people? I simply cannot live without other people<br /> <br />He heard a noise. It was a knock<br /> <br />He recognized it as the rush of rain<br /> <br />Only when he was at the top, approaching the seventh floor, did he suddenly become frightened by the end of his journey: up there the pigeon was waiting, that ghastly animal<br /> <br />The hall was completely empty. The pigeon had vanished</span>

<div id="review_149713008" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149713008" />
    <a title="harryknuckles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4815676-harryknuckles"><img alt="harryknuckles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552380468p2/4815676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149713008?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="harryknuckles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="harryknuckles" href="/user/show/4815676-harryknuckles">harryknuckles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149713008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1140484329931216943">The Pigeon ~ Patrick Süskind<br/><br/><br/><b>The Pigeon</b> is a strange little tale in many ways. In only 77 pages Süskind gives you a rather unusual and momentous day in the life of Jonathan Noel.<br/><br/>In the first five pages you have pretty much been given his life story. After a rocky childhood as a result of World War II, Jonathan Noel had a good thirty years of mundane existence. He has been renting a room for that time and even though he does not even have a private bathroom he has decided to buy it. He works as do</span>
  <span id="freeText1140484329931216943" style="display:none">The Pigeon ~ Patrick Süskind<br /><br /><br /><b>The Pigeon</b> is a strange little tale in many ways. In only 77 pages Süskind gives you a rather unusual and momentous day in the life of Jonathan Noel.<br /><br />In the first five pages you have pretty much been given his life story. After a rocky childhood as a result of World War II, Jonathan Noel had a good thirty years of mundane existence. He has been renting a room for that time and even though he does not even have a private bathroom he has decided to buy it. He works as doorman at a bank and day after day he follows the exact same schedule. But now that he is in his fifties, things are about to change. <br /><br />One Friday morning in the month of August 1984, while on his way to the bathroom, Jonathan sees a pigeon outside the door of his room and goes into panic. He is afraid that pigeons will overtake the apartment and he does not dare kill it. This event causes Jonathan to have a complete nervous breakdown, to both comic and emotional extremes, which seems to have been waiting to happen for forty or so years. Jonathan embarks in a series of crazy plans to evade the object that causes his strain, going as far as moving into a hotel, even though he cannot afford it.<br /><br />As Jonathan's day slowly got worse I found myself both laughing more and worrying more all at once. I also really empathised with him, due to the skill in which Süskind conveys his emotions and frustrations. When he rips his trousers you are completely there with him, you know how it feels: the humiliation, the anger at the trousers and then at yourself, it's very well done. <br /><br />I don't think you can compare any two works of one author especially when one is as successful as <b>Perfume</b>. I can say that <b>The Pigeon</b> has reminded me why I love <b>Perfume</b> so much. Once more Süskind shows his ability for delving into the psyche of his characters and providing his readers with awesome insight. He can also take you to beautiful cities like Paris and darken them with his words without making anything grandly gothic or overdone. It will also have you wondering what small incident or happening could change your life forever. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1140484329931216943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129659958" />
    <a title="Al Bità" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit"><img alt="Al Bità" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483528143p2/1533440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129659958?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Al Bità" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Al Bità" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit">Al Bità</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129659958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2790491844492186452">This is a short work, 77 pages long, and using largish type. It is by the author of the brilliant novel 'Perfume', and just as in that novel the author allows the reader to enter almost seamlessly and painlessly into the mind of an obsessive psychotic murderer, here the author eases us into the mind of a psychologically damaged individual. In both cases, we can say that we as readers have experienced what it must be like to be the central character. The origin of the psychological damage is not </span>
  <span id="freeText2790491844492186452" style="display:none">This is a short work, 77 pages long, and using largish type. It is by the author of the brilliant novel 'Perfume', and just as in that novel the author allows the reader to enter almost seamlessly and painlessly into the mind of an obsessive psychotic murderer, here the author eases us into the mind of a psychologically damaged individual. In both cases, we can say that we as readers have experienced what it must be like to be the central character. The origin of the psychological damage is not specifically stated, but it occurs in France during the Second World War, and since first the mother, then the father 'disappear', and the children are sent south so that they could be better hidden until the end of the war, we can surmise that Jonathan Noel is either of Jewish background, or that the family was one which for some other reason might have been threatened by the situation in France in 1942.<br /><br />Jonathan is soon alone, follows the orders of others, who ultimately disabuse him, and finally he retreats into his closed-off world, alone, isolated, and apparently basically 'happy' in his 30-year job as a bank security guard. It takes only a very simple ordinary incident, however, to have this protected, sheltered world shattered almost to the point of destruction.<br /><br />I'm not sure how others might respond to this obsessive, claustrophobic world, but I think Süskind's ability to make us enter this highly protected mind is skilfully done. I feel the reader is made to understand something about the devastating effects of trauma, and to appreciate just how delicate the fabric of such damaged souls is. It is both moving and frightening at the same time, so quintessentially disturbing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2790491844492186452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Indeed, over these long hours, it had transformed him into the impotent, menacing form of a sphinx. It was something like the electrical tension that magnetises a lump of iron and holds it suspended, or the powerful strenth in the vault of an edifice that grips each individual stone firmly in its spell. It was subjunctive. Its whole potential rested in "I would, I could, most of all I'd like to", and as he formulated these </span>
  <span id="freeText3019282238938611841" style="display:none">(a part i liked - SPOILER ALERT kind of)<br /><br />The force turned him into stone. Indeed, over these long hours, it had transformed him into the impotent, menacing form of a sphinx. It was something like the electrical tension that magnetises a lump of iron and holds it suspended, or the powerful strenth in the vault of an edifice that grips each individual stone firmly in its spell. It was subjunctive. Its whole potential rested in "I would, I could, most of all I'd like to", and as he formulated these horrible subjunctive curses in his mind, Jonathan also knew very well that he would never act on them. He was not the man for that. He was not a man to run amok, to commit crime by anguish of soul or spontaneous hatred; and not because for him such a crime seemed a moral abomination, but for the simple reason that he was totally incapable of *asserting* himself in either word or deed. He was not a man of action. He was a man of resignation. <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8085925962086652890">First, a huge thank you to Jacob, who sent me a spare copy of this lovely novella.<br/><br/>Suskind throws an obsessive, habit-bound man into a silly situation that sparks an existential crisis. "Pigeon" read like a 19th century novel of similar ilk, such as "Notes from the Underground." I appreciate this kind of literary technique, and I love Suskind's prose. If he ever writes another novel, I will read it.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1758581854" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1758581854" />
    <a title="Tamsien West (Babbling Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6390827-tamsien-west-babbling-books"><img alt="Tamsien West (Babbling Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540116596p2/6390827.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1758581854?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamsien West (Babbling Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamsien West (Babbling Books)" href="/user/show/6390827-tamsien-west-babbling-books">Tamsien West (Babbling Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141045269" href="/book/show/7737084-the-pigeon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6390827-tamsien-west-babbling-books?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1758581854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1422121688152816315">The Pigeon is a truly strange and vividly intense novella. The narrative covers a single 24 hour period in the life of a Frenchman, with every action and emotion expressed in graphic, almost violent, prose.<br/><br/>This is not the kind of story that is particularly enjoyable or relaxing to read, because it is so overwhelming, but it is a really fascinating example of the potential of literature. The protagonist’s identity is almost completely obscured by the paranoia and intense emotions he experiences a</span>
  <span id="freeText1422121688152816315" style="display:none">The Pigeon is a truly strange and vividly intense novella. The narrative covers a single 24 hour period in the life of a Frenchman, with every action and emotion expressed in graphic, almost violent, prose.<br /><br />This is not the kind of story that is particularly enjoyable or relaxing to read, because it is so overwhelming, but it is a really fascinating example of the potential of literature. The protagonist’s identity is almost completely obscured by the paranoia and intense emotions he experiences and the dramatic way in which small details are expressed.<br /><br /><i>“A pigeon is the epitome of chaos and anarchy”</i><br /><br />There are some truly brilliant passages throughout, that deal with everything from humanity’s fickle and fleeting existence to terror felt when a hole is torn in one’s pants while in public.<br /><br /><i>“so titanic was Jonathan Noel’s hate that afternoon, that he would have liked to reduce the world to rubble and ashes, because he had a hole in his trousers” </i><br /><br />It’s difficult to imagine what would lead a writer to having a character believe a simple clap of thunder heralds the apocalypse. There are echoes of unresolved abandonment, desire for order and rigidity in the face of a past filled with chaos, but at the same time it’s a story about a man whose day is disrupted by a pigeon.<br /><br />Finishing this novella is like breathing in a huge gulp of fresh air after a long time in a cramped and stuffy elevator. The rain that falls on the last few pages forms a symbolic ‘washing away’ of the pigeon trauma, and also cleanses readers of the anxiety and paranoia that escalated throughout the story.<br /><br /><i>“All at once he was no longer afraid”</i><br /><br />I can’t say that I particularly enjoyed reading this book, but nor can I say that I regret reading it. Suskind’s writing style is so intriguing that I will definitely be looking out for more of his work. I would recommend this if you have a short trip via plane, train or bus, and can sit for the hour and a half that it would take to read this in a single sitting. Despite the surface simplicity of the tale it’s one that keeps coming back to me since finishing it, little sentences here and there jumping out at me, so in that sense it is truly a memorable read.<br /><br />Suitable for ages 15 and up, but probably best enjoyed by readers who have been employed in at least one truly tedious job.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1422121688152816315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10742531738427163961" style="display:none">There's something about a Novella that magnifies the need for consideration. They cannot be fully appreciated on a surface level, but somehow seem always to be much deeper than their word count insinuates.<br />This book for me was a gorgeous example of the phenomena by which an insignificant event can compound upon your soul. One small unexpected thing can throw off your balance, and seems to invite further chaos, pain and suffering. A bad day tips you to the point of spiralling into existentialism and nihilism.<br />On the surface, this seems like a silly story about a man who completely overreacts to the presence of a Pigeon at his doorway. But really, is a powerful portrayal of the dangerous workings of the unchecked mind.<br />Those who know me well will also appreciate my glee that the chaos ensues from a literal Feather, unexpectedly delivered to an unsuspecting man from our benefactor, The Universe!!😂</span>

<div id="review_164470867" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164470867" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5241822-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411759051p2/5241822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164470867?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/5241822-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5241822-david?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164470867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13524780580732693331">I just reread this book for probably the fourth or fifth time, and I was reminded again of what a remarkable, complex, insightful book this is - and all in 115 pages that can be read in a couple of hours. <br/><br/>Two sentences tell you everything you need to know about Jonathan Noel's life up to the action in this book:<br/><br/>"For he was not fond of events, and hated outright those that rattled his inner equilibrium and made a muddle of the external arrangements of life." <br/><br/>And, regarding the one small room he h</span>
  <span id="freeText13524780580732693331" style="display:none">I just reread this book for probably the fourth or fifth time, and I was reminded again of what a remarkable, complex, insightful book this is - and all in 115 pages that can be read in a couple of hours. <br /><br />Two sentences tell you everything you need to know about Jonathan Noel's life up to the action in this book:<br /><br />"For he was not fond of events, and hated outright those that rattled his inner equilibrium and made a muddle of the external arrangements of life." <br /><br />And, regarding the one small room he had lived in for 30 years...<br /><br />"He was not looking for comfort, but for secure lodgings that belonged to him and him alone, that protected him against life's unpleasant surprises and from which no one could ever drive him away again."<br /><br />Doesn't sound like a very sympathetic character that would make for a good story, does it? And yet the reasons (good ones) for Jonathan's outlook are accounted for in the early pages, and when he encounters The Pigeon in the hallway outside his door one morning, his carefully crafted, safe existence threatens to crumble. It is amazing to see how this one event threatens to shatter Jonathan's life, his sense of self. It's sad, funny, and totally believable to anyone who has ever kept life at arm's length for whatever reason, only to have external factors invade their comfort zone. Following the pigeon's appearance, a steady cascade of events signal to Jonathan that his life is spiraling out of control. This is presented in an entirely believable way; the psychological insight present in this book is extremely astute.<br /><br />Will Jonathan in fact end up losing everything he holds dear? His job, his home, his very sense of self? Does the pigeon signal the end of life as he knows it? Is there any hope for a resolution of this inner conflict? Invest a couple of hours in reading this amazing book and find out!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13524780580732693331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
